<p>The <b><a href="/wiki/History_of_Libya" title="History of Libya">history of Libya</a> under <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Muammar Gaddafi</a></b> spanned a period of over four decades from 1969 to 2011. Gaddafi became the <i>de facto</i> leader of the country on 1 September 1969 after leading a group of young Libyan military officers against <a href="/wiki/Idris_of_Libya" title="Idris of Libya">King Idris I</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Nonviolent_revolution" title="Nonviolent revolution">bloodless coup d'état</a>. After the king had fled the country, the <a href="/wiki/Libyan_Revolutionary_Command_Council" title="Libyan Revolutionary Command Council">Libyan Revolutionary Command Council</a> (RCC) headed by Gaddafi abolished the monarchy and the old constitution and proclaimed the new Libyan Arab Republic, with the motto "freedom, socialism, and unity".<sup id="cite_ref-GlobalEdge_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GlobalEdge-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After coming to power, the RCC government initiated a process of directing funds toward providing education, health care and housing for all. Despite the reforms not being entirely effective, public education in the country became free and primary education compulsory for both sexes. Medical care became available to the public at no cost but providing housing for all was a task the RCC government was not able to complete.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> Under Gaddafi, per capita income in the country rose to more than US $11,000, the fifth highest in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> The increase in prosperity was accompanied by a controversial foreign policy, with increased political repression at home.<sup id="cite_ref-GlobalEdge_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GlobalEdge-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>During the 1980s and 1990s, Gaddafi openly supported rebel movements like <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a>'s <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress" title="African National Congress">African National Congress</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Palestine_Liberation_Organization" title="Palestine Liberation Organization">Palestine Liberation Organization</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Republican_Army" title="Irish Republican Army">Irish Republican Army</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Polisario_Front" title="Polisario Front">Polisario Front</a> (<a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Sahara</a>), which led to a deterioration of <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Libya" title="Foreign relations of Libya">Libya's foreign relations</a> with several countries and that culminated in the <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Libya_(1986)" title="Bombing of Libya (1986)" class="mw-redirect">US bombing of Libya in 1986</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">9/11 attacks</a>, however, the relations were mostly normalised.</p>
<p>In early 2011, a <a href="/wiki/2011_Libyan_Civil_War" title="2011 Libyan Civil War" class="mw-redirect">civil war</a> broke out in the context of the wider "<a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a>". The <a href="/wiki/Anti-Gaddafi_forces" title="Anti-Gaddafi forces">anti-Gaddafi forces</a> formed a committee named the <a href="/wiki/National_Transitional_Council" title="National Transitional Council">National Transitional Council</a>, on 27 February 2011. It was meant to act as an interim authority in the rebel-controlled areas. After a number of atrocities were committed by the government,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> with the threat of further bloodshed,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/2011_military_intervention_in_Libya" title="2011 military intervention in Libya">multinational coalition</a> led by <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> forces intervened on 21 March 2011 with the aim to protect civilians against attacks by the government's forces.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> At the same time, the <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Court" title="International Criminal Court">International Criminal Court</a> issued an arrest warrant against Gaddafi and his entourage on 27 June 2011. Gaddafi was ousted from power in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tripoli_(2011)" title="Battle of Tripoli (2011)">fall of Tripoli</a> to the rebel forces on 20 August 2011, although pockets of resistance held by forces loyal to Gaddafi's government held out for another two months, especially in Gaddafi's hometown of <a href="/wiki/Sirte" title="Sirte">Sirte</a>, which he declared the new capital of Libya on 1 September 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-sirte_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sirte-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> The fall of the last remaining cities under pro-Gaddafi control and Sirte's capture on 20 October 2011, followed by the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Death of Muammar Gaddafi">killing of Gaddafi</a>, marked the end of the Libyan Arab Jamahiriya.</p>
<p></p>

</li>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The name of the country was changed several times during Gaddafi's tenure as the leader. At first, the name was the <b>Libyan Arab Republic</b>. In 1977, the name was changed to <b>Socialist People's Libyan Arab Jamahiriya</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-P.-M._Favre_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P.-M._Favre-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Jamahiriya" title="Jamahiriya" class="mw-redirect">Jamahiriya</a></i> was a term coined by Gaddafi,<sup id="cite_ref-P.-M._Favre_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-P.-M._Favre-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> usually translated as "state of the masses".</p>
<p>The country was renamed again in 1986 as the <b>Great Socialist People's Libyan Arab Jamahiriya</b>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coup_d.27.C3.A9tat_of_1969">Coup d'état of 1969</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Coup d'état of 1969">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1969_Libyan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1969 Libyan coup d'état">1969 Libyan coup d'état</a></div>
<p>The discovery of significant <a href="/wiki/Oil_reserves" title="Oil reserves">oil reserves</a> in 1959 and the subsequent income from <a href="/wiki/Libyan_oil_industry" title="Libyan oil industry" class="mw-redirect">petroleum sales</a> enabled the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Libya" title="Kingdom of Libya">Kingdom of Libya</a> to transition from one of the world's poorest nations to a wealthy state. Although oil drastically improved the Libyan government's finances, resentment began to build over the increased concentration of the nation's wealth in the hands of King Idris. This discontent mounted with the rise of <a href="/wiki/Nasserism" title="Nasserism">Nasserism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arab_nationalism" title="Arab nationalism">Arab nationalism</a>/<a href="/wiki/Arab_socialism" title="Arab socialism">socialism</a> throughout North Africa and the Middle East.</p>
<p>On 1 September 1969, a group of about 70 young army officers known as the Free Officers Movement and enlisted men mostly assigned to the <a href="/wiki/Military_communications" title="Military communications">Signal Corps</a>, seized control of the government and in a stroke abolished the Libyan monarchy. The coup was launched at <a href="/wiki/Benghazi" title="Benghazi">Benghazi</a>, and within two hours the takeover was completed. Army units quickly rallied in support of the coup, and within a few days firmly established military control in Tripoli and elsewhere throughout the country. Popular reception of the coup, especially by younger people in the urban areas, was enthusiastic. Fears of resistance in <a href="/wiki/Cyrenaica" title="Cyrenaica">Cyrenaica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fezzan" title="Fezzan">Fezzan</a> proved unfounded. No deaths or violent incidents related to the coup were reported.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Free Officers Movement, which claimed credit for carrying out the coup, was headed by a twelve-member directorate that designated itself the Revolutionary Command Council (RCC). This body constituted the Libyan government after the coup. In its initial proclamation on 1 September,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> the RCC declared the country to be a free and sovereign state called the <b>Libyan Arab Republic</b>, which would proceed "in the path of freedom, unity, and social justice, guaranteeing the right of equality to its citizens, and opening before them the doors of honorable work." The rule of the Turks and Italians and the "reactionary" government just overthrown were characterized as belonging to "dark ages", from which the Libyan people were called to move forward as "free brothers" to a new age of prosperity, equality, and honor.</p>
<p>The RCC advised diplomatic representatives in Libya that the revolutionary changes had not been directed from outside the country, that existing treaties and agreements would remain in effect, and that foreign lives and property would be protected. Diplomatic recognition of the new government came quickly from countries throughout the world. United States recognition was officially extended on 6 September.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-coup">Post-coup</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Post-coup">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nasser_Gaddafi_1969.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Nasser_Gaddafi_1969.jpg/220px-Nasser_Gaddafi_1969.jpg" width="220" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c1/Nasser_Gaddafi_1969.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c1/Nasser_Gaddafi_1969.jpg 2x" data-file-width="321" data-file-height="400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nasser_Gaddafi_1969.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gaddafi (left) with Egyptian President <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a> in 1969.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In view of the lack of internal resistance, it appeared that the chief danger to the new government lay in the possibility of a reaction inspired by the absent King Idris or his designated heir, <a href="/wiki/Hasan_as-Senussi" title="Hasan as-Senussi">Hasan ar Rida</a>, who had been taken into custody at the time of the coup along with other senior civil and military officials of the royal government. Within days of the coup, however, Hasan publicly renounced all rights to the throne, stated his support for the new government, and called on the people to accept it without violence.</p>
<p>Idris, in an exchange of messages with the RCC through Egypt's President <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Nasser</a>, dissociated himself from reported attempts to secure British intervention and disclaimed any intention of coming back to Libya. In return, he was assured by the RCC of the safety of his family still in the country. At his own request and with Nasser's approval, Idris took up residence once again in Egypt, where he had spent his first exile and where he remained until his death in 1983.</p>
<p>On 7 September 1969, the RCC announced that it had appointed a cabinet to conduct the government of the new republic. An American-educated technician, <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_Sulayman_al-Maghribi" title="Mahmud Sulayman al-Maghribi">Mahmud Sulayman al-Maghribi</a>, who had been imprisoned since 1967 for his political activities, was designated prime minister. He presided over the eight-member Council of Ministers, of whom six, like Maghrabi, were civilians and two – <b>Adam Said Hawwaz</b> and <b>Musa Ahmad</b> – were military officers. Neither of the officers was a member of the RCC.</p>
<p>The Council of Ministers was instructed to "implement the state's general policy as drawn up by the RCC", leaving no doubt where ultimate authority rested. The next day the RCC decided to promote Captain Gaddafi to colonel and to appoint him commander in chief of the Libyan Armed Forces. Although RCC spokesmen declined until January 1970 to reveal any other names of RCC members, it was apparent from that date onward that the head of the RCC and new <i>de facto</i> head of state was Gaddafi.</p>
<p>Analysts were quick to point out the striking similarities between the Libyan military coup of 1969 and that in Egypt under Nasser in 1952, and it became clear that the Egyptian experience and the charismatic figure of Nasser had formed the model for the Free Officers Movement. As the RCC in the last months of 1969 moved vigorously to institute domestic reforms, it proclaimed neutrality in the confrontation between the superpowers and opposition to all forms of colonialism and "imperialism". It also made clear Libya's dedication to Arab unity and to the support of the Palestinian cause against Israel.</p>
<p>The RCC reaffirmed the country's identity as part of the "Arab nation" and its state religion as <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>. It abolished parliamentary institutions, all legislative functions being assumed by the RCC, and continued the prohibition against political parties, in effect since 1952. The new government categorically rejected communism – in large part because it was <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheist</a> – and officially espoused an Arab interpretation of socialism that integrated Islamic principles with social, economic, and political reform. Libya had shifted, virtually overnight, from the camp of conservative Arab traditionalist states to that of the radical nationalist states.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Libyan_Arab_Republic_.281969.E2.80.931977.29">Libyan Arab Republic (1969–1977) <span id="Libyan_Arab_Republic"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Libyan Arab Republic (1969–1977)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attempted_counter-coups">Attempted counter-coups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Attempted counter-coups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the formation of the Libyan Arab Republic, Gaddafi and his associates insisted that their government would not rest on individual leadership, but rather on collegial decision making.</p>
<p>The first major cabinet change occurred soon after the first challenge to the government. In December 1969, Adam Said Hawwaz, the minister of defense, and Musa Ahmad, the minister of interior, were arrested and accused of planning a coup. In the new cabinet formed after the crisis, Gaddafi, retaining his post as chairman of the RCC, also became prime minister and defense minister.<sup id="cite_ref-countrystudies_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-countrystudies-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Abdessalam_Jalloud" title="Abdessalam Jalloud">Major Abdel Salam Jallud</a>, generally regarded as second only to Gaddafi in the RCC, became deputy prime minister and minister of interior.<sup id="cite_ref-countrystudies_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-countrystudies-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> This cabinet totaled thirteen members, of whom five were RCC officers.<sup id="cite_ref-countrystudies_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-countrystudies-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> The government was challenged a second time in July 1970 when Abdullah Abid Sanusi and <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_al-Senussi" title="Ahmed al-Senussi">Ahmed al-Senussi</a>, distant cousins of former King Idris, and members of the Sayf an Nasr clan of Fezzan were accused of plotting to seize power for themselves.<sup id="cite_ref-countrystudies_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-countrystudies-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> After the plot was foiled, a substantial cabinet change occurred, RCC officers for the first time forming a majority among new ministers.<sup id="cite_ref-countrystudies_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-countrystudies-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assertion_of_Gaddafi.27s_control">Assertion of Gaddafi's control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Assertion of Gaddafi's control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From the start, RCC spokesmen had indicated a serious intent to bring the "defunct regime" to account. In 1971 and 1972 more than 200 former government officials—including seven prime ministers and numerous cabinet ministers—as well as former King Idris and members of the royal family, were brought to trial on charges of treason and corruption in the <a href="/wiki/Libyan_People%27s_Court" title="Libyan People's Court">Libyan People's Court</a>.</p>
<p>Many, who like Idris lived in exile, were <a href="/wiki/Trial_in_absentia" title="Trial in absentia">tried <i>in absentia</i></a>. Although a large percentage of those charged were acquitted, sentences of up to fifteen years in prison and heavy fines were imposed on others. Five death sentences, all but one of them <i>in absentia</i>, were pronounced, among them, one against Idris. <a href="/wiki/Queen_Fatima" title="Queen Fatima" class="mw-redirect">Fatima</a>, the former queen, and Hasan ar Rida were sentenced to five and three years in prison, respectively.</p>
<p>Meanwhile, Gaddafi and the RCC had disbanded the <a href="/wiki/Sanusi_order" title="Sanusi order" class="mw-redirect">Sanusi order</a> and officially downgraded its historical role in achieving Libya's independence. He also attacked regional and tribal differences as obstructions in the path of social advancement and Arab unity, dismissing traditional leaders and drawing administrative boundaries across <a href="/wiki/Tribes_of_Libya" title="Tribes of Libya" class="mw-redirect">tribal groupings</a>.</p>
<p>The Free Officers Movement was renamed "<a href="/wiki/Arab_Socialist_Union_(Libya)" title="Arab Socialist Union (Libya)" class="mw-redirect">Arab Socialist Union</a>" (ASU) in 1971, modeled after Egypt's <a href="/wiki/Arab_Socialist_Union_(Egypt)" title="Arab Socialist Union (Egypt)">Arab Socialist Union</a>, and made the <a href="/wiki/Single-party_state" title="Single-party state">sole legal party</a> in Gaddafi's Libya. It acted as a "vehicle of national expression", purporting to "raise the political consciousness of Libyans" and to "aid the RCC in formulating public policy through debate in open forums".<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> Trade unions were incorporated into the ASU and strikes outlawed. The press, already subject to censorship, was officially conscripted in 1972 as an agent of the revolution. Italians and what remained of the Jewish community were expelled from the country and their property confiscated in October 1970.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Libya_(1969%E2%80%931972).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Flag_of_Libya_%281969%E2%80%931972%29.svg/250px-Flag_of_Libya_%281969%E2%80%931972%29.svg.png" width="250" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Flag_of_Libya_%281969%E2%80%931972%29.svg/375px-Flag_of_Libya_%281969%E2%80%931972%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Flag_of_Libya_%281969%E2%80%931972%29.svg/500px-Flag_of_Libya_%281969%E2%80%931972%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

The <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Libya" title="Flag of Libya">Flag</a> of the Libyan Arab Republic (1969–1972).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_arms_of_Libya-1970.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Coat_of_arms_of_Libya-1970.svg/250px-Coat_of_arms_of_Libya-1970.svg.png" width="250" height="340" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Coat_of_arms_of_Libya-1970.svg/375px-Coat_of_arms_of_Libya-1970.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Coat_of_arms_of_Libya-1970.svg/500px-Coat_of_arms_of_Libya-1970.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="443" data-file-height="602" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_arms_of_Libya-1970.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Libya" title="Coat of arms of Libya">Coat</a> of the Libyan Arab Republic (1969–1972).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1972, Libya joined the <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Arab_Republics" title="Federation of Arab Republics">Federation of Arab Republics</a> with <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> but the intended union of pan-Arabic states never had the intended success, and was effectively dormant after 1973.</p>
<p>As months passed, Gaddafi, caught up in his <a href="/wiki/Apocalypticism" title="Apocalypticism">apocalyptic</a> visions of revolutionary pan-Arabism and Islam locked in mortal struggle with what he termed the encircling, demonic forces of reaction, imperialism, and Zionism, increasingly devoted attention to international rather than internal affairs. As a result, routine administrative tasks fell to Major Jallud, who in 1972 became prime minister in place of Gaddafi. Two years later Jallud assumed Gaddafi's remaining administrative and protocol duties to allow Gaddafi to devote his time to revolutionary theorizing. Gaddafi remained commander in chief of the armed forces and effective head of state. The foreign press speculated about an eclipse of his authority and personality within the RCC, but Gaddafi soon dispelled such theories by his measures to restructure Libyan society.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alignment_with_the_Soviet_bloc">Alignment with the Soviet bloc</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Alignment with the Soviet bloc">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the September coup, U.S. forces proceeded deliberately with the planned withdrawal from <a href="/wiki/Wheelus_Air_Base" title="Wheelus Air Base">Wheelus Air Base</a> under the agreement made with the previous government. The last of the American contingent turned the facility over to the Libyans on 11 June 1970, a date thereafter celebrated in Libya as a national holiday.</p>
<p>As relations with the U.S. steadily deteriorated, Gaddafi forged close links with the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc" title="Eastern Bloc">Eastern Bloc</a> countries, all the while maintaining Libya's stance as a nonaligned country and opposing the spread of communism in the Arab world. Libya's army—sharply increased from the 6,000-man prerevolutionary force that had been trained and equipped by the British—was armed with Soviet-built armor and missiles.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Petroleum_politics">Petroleum politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Petroleum politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The economic base for Libya's revolution has been its oil revenues. However, Libya's petroleum reserves were small compared with those of other major Arab petroleum-producing states. As a consequence, Libya was more ready to ration output in order to conserve its natural wealth and less responsive to moderating its price-rise demands than the other countries. Petroleum was seen both as a means of financing the economic and social development of a woefully underdeveloped country and as a political weapon to brandish in the Arab struggle against Israel.</p>
<p>The increase in production that followed the 1969 revolution was accompanied by Libyan demands for higher petroleum prices, a greater share of revenues, and more control over the development of the country's petroleum industry. Foreign petroleum companies agreed to a price hike of more than three times the going rate (from US$0.90 to US$3.45 per barrel) early in 1971. In December, the Libyan government suddenly nationalized the holdings of <a href="/wiki/British_Petroleum" title="British Petroleum" class="mw-redirect">British Petroleum</a> in Libya and withdrew funds amounting to approximately US$550 million invested in British banks as a result of a foreign policy dispute. British Petroleum rejected as inadequate a Libyan offer of compensation, and the British treasury banned Libya from participation in the sterling area.</p>
<p>In 1973, the Libyan government announced the nationalization of a controlling interest in all other petroleum companies operating in the country. This step gave Libya control of about 60 percent of its domestic oil production by early 1974, a figure that subsequently rose to 70 percent. Total nationalization was out of the question, given the need for foreign expertise and funds in oil exploration, production, and distribution.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1973_oil_crisis">1973 oil crisis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 1973 oil crisis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Insisting on the continued use of petroleum as leverage against Israel and its supporters in the West, Libya strongly urged the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries (<a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a>) to <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">take action in 1973</a>, and Libyan militancy was partially responsible for OPEC measures to raise oil prices, impose embargoes, and gain control of production. On 19 October 1973, Libya was the first Arab nation to issue an oil embargo against the United States after US President Richard Nixon announced the US would provide Israel with a $2.2 billion military aid program during the <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War" title="Yom Kippur War">Yom Kippur War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gaddafiem_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gaddafiem-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> Saudi Arabia and other Arab oil producing nations in OPEC would follow suit the next day.<sup id="cite_ref-gaddafiem_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gaddafiem-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>While the other Arab nations lifted their oil embargoes on 18 March 1974,<sup id="cite_ref-gaddafiem_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gaddafiem-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> the Gaddafi regime refused to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> As a consequence of such policies, Libya's oil production declined by half between 1970 and 1974, while revenues from oil exports more than quadrupled. Production continued to fall, bottoming out at an eleven-year low in 1975 at a time when the government was preparing to invest large amounts of petroleum revenues in other sectors of the economy. Thereafter, output stabilized at about two million barrels per day. Production and hence income declined yet again in the early 1980s because of the high price of Libyan crude and because recession in the industrialized world reduced demand for oil from all sources.</p>
<p>Libya's Five-Year Economic and Social Transformation Plan (1976–80), announced in 1975, was programmed to pump US$20 billion into the development of a broad range of economic activities that would continue to provide income after Libya's petroleum reserves had been exhausted. Agriculture was slated to receive the largest share of aid in an effort to make Libya self-sufficient in food and to help keep the rural population on the land. Industry, of which there was little before the revolution, also received a significant amount of funding in the first development plan as well as in the second, launched in 1981.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transition_to_the_Jamahiriya_.281973.E2.80.931977.29">Transition to the <i>Jamahiriya</i> (1973–1977)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Transition to the Jamahiriya (1973–1977)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alfateh_2010.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Alfateh_2010.jpg/220px-Alfateh_2010.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Alfateh_2010.jpg/330px-Alfateh_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Alfateh_2010.jpg/440px-Alfateh_2010.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alfateh_2010.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
(Alfateh, 1 September 1969) Festivity Alfateh in <a href="/wiki/Bayda,_Libya" title="Bayda, Libya">Bayda</a>, Libya in 1 September 2010.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/The_Green_Book_(Libya)" title="The Green Book (Libya)" class="mw-redirect">The Green Book (Libya)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Third_International_Theory" title="Third International Theory">Third International Theory</a></div>
<p>The "remaking of Libyan society" contained in Gaddafi's ideological visions began to be put into practice formally in 1973, with a so-called cultural or popular revolution. This revolution was designed to create bureaucratic efficiency, public interest and participation in the subnational governmental system, and national political coordination. In an attempt to instill revolutionary fervor into his compatriots and to involve large numbers of them in political affairs, Gaddafi urged them to challenge traditional authority and to take over and run government organs themselves. The instrument for doing this was the people's committee. Within a few months, such committees were found all across Libya. They were functionally and geographically based, and eventually became responsible for local and regional administration.</p>
<p>People's committees were established in such widely divergent organizations as universities, private business firms, government bureaucracies, and the broadcast media. Geographically based committees were formed at the governorate, municipal, and zone (lowest) levels. Seats on the people's committees at the zone level were filled by direct popular election; members so elected could then be selected for service at higher levels. By mid-1973 estimates of the number of people's committees ranged above 2,000.</p>
<p>In the scope of their administrative and regulatory tasks and the method of their members' selection, the people's committees purportedly embodied the concept of <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a> that Gaddafi propounded in the first volume of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Green_Book_(Libya)" title="The Green Book (Libya)" class="mw-redirect">The Green Book</a></i>, which appeared in 1976. The same concept lay behind proposals to create a new political structure composed of "people's congresses." The centerpiece of the new system was the <a href="/wiki/General_People%27s_Congress_(Libya)" title="General People's Congress (Libya)">General People's Congress</a> (GPC), a national representative body intended to replace the RCC.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Socialist_People.27s_Libyan_Arab_Jamahiriya_.281977.E2.80.932011.29">Great Socialist People's Libyan Arab Jamahiriya (1977–2011)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Great Socialist People's Libyan Arab Jamahiriya (1977–2011)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</table>
<p>On 2 March 1977, the GPC, at Gaddafi's behest, adopted the "Declaration of the Establishment of the People's Authority"<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> and proclaimed the <b>Socialist People's Libyan Arab Jamahiriya</b> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language" class="mw-redirect">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">الجماهيرية العربية الليبية الشعبية الاشتراكية</span>‎<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> <i><span title="Arabic transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">al-Jamāhīrīyah al-‘Arabīyah al-Lībīyah ash-Sha‘bīyah al-Ishtirākīyah</span></i>). In the official political philosophy of Gaddafi's state, the "Jamahiriya" system was unique to the country, although it was presented as the materialization of the <a href="/wiki/Third_International_Theory" title="Third International Theory">Third International Theory</a>, proposed by Gaddafi to be applied to the entire <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a>.</p>
<p>Gaddafi was designated the "Leader" (<i><span title="Arabic transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">Qā’id</span></i>) of the Libyan state and was accorded the honorifics "Brotherly Leader and Guide to the First of September Great Revolution of the Great Socialist People's Libyan Arab Jamahiriya".</p>
<p>The Libyan government stated that the Libyan Jamahiriya was a <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a> <a href="/wiki/Nonpartisan" title="Nonpartisan" class="mw-redirect">without any political parties</a>, governed by its populace through local popular councils and communes (named <a href="/wiki/Basic_People%27s_Congress_(political)" title="Basic People's Congress (political)">Basic People's Congresses</a>). Official rhetoric disdained the idea of a <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation state</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tribal" title="Tribal" class="mw-redirect">tribal</a> bonds remaining primary, even within the ranks of the <a href="/wiki/Armed_forces_of_the_Libyan_Arab_Jamahiriya" title="Armed forces of the Libyan Arab Jamahiriya" class="mw-redirect">military of Libya</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span id="Jamahiriya"></span> <b>Jamahiriya</b> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language" class="mw-redirect">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">جماهيرية</span>‎ <i><span title="Arabic transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">jamāhīrīyah</span></i>) is an <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language" class="mw-redirect">Arabic</a> term generally translated as "state of the masses"; Lisa Anderson <sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> has suggested "peopledom" or "state of the masses" as a reasonable approximations of the meaning of the term as intended by Gaddafi. The term does not occur in this sense in <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Muammar Gaddafi</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Green_Book_(Libya)" title="The Green Book (Libya)" class="mw-redirect">Green Book</a></i> of 1975. The <a href="/wiki/Arabic_grammar" title="Arabic grammar">nisba</a>-adjective <i><span title="Arabic transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">jamāhīrīyah</span></i> ("mass-, "of the masses") occurs only in the third part, published in 1981, in the phrase <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">إن الحركات التاريخية هي الحركات الجماهيرية</span> (<i><span title="Arabic transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">Inna al-ḥarakāt at-tārīkhīyah hiya al-ḥarakāt al-jamāhīrīyah</span></i>), translated in the English edition as "Historic movements are mass movements".</p>
<p>The word <i><span title="Arabic transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">jamāhīrīyah</span></i> was derived from <i><span title="Arabic transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><a href="/wiki/Jumhuriya_(disambiguation)" title="Jumhuriya (disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect">jumhūrīyah</a></span>,</i> which is the usual Arabic translation of "republic". It was coined by changing the component <i><span title="Arabic transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">jumhūr</span></i> — "public" — to its plural form, <i><span title="Arabic transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">jamāhīr</span></i> — "the masses". Thus, it is similar to the term <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic" title="People's Republic">People's Republic</a>. It is often left untranslated in English, with the long-form name thus rendered as <i>Great Socialist People's Libyan Arab Jamahiriya.</i></p>
<p>After weathering the <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Libya_(1986)" title="Bombing of Libya (1986)" class="mw-redirect">1986 bombing</a> by the Reagan administration, Gaddafi added the specifier "Great" (<span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">العظمى</span> <i><span title="Arabic transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">al-‘Uẓmá</span></i>) to the official name of the country.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reforms_.281977.E2.80.931980.29">Reforms (1977–1980)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Reforms (1977–1980)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaddafi_as_permanent_.22Leader_of_the_Revolution.22">Gaddafi as permanent "Leader of the Revolution"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Gaddafi as permanent &quot;Leader of the Revolution&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The changes in Libyan leadership since 1976 culminated in March 1979, when the GPC declared that the "vesting of power in the masses" and the "separation of the state from the revolution" were complete. Gaddafi relinquished his duties as general secretary of the GPC, being known thereafter as "the leader" or "Leader of the Revolution." He remained supreme commander of the armed forces. His replacement was Abdallah Ubaydi, who in effect had been prime minister since 1979.</p>
<p>The GPC also adopted resolutions designating Gaddafi as its general secretary and creating the General Secretariat of the GPC, comprising the remaining members of the defunct RCC. It also appointed the General People's Committee, which replaced the Council of Ministers, its members now called secretaries rather than ministers.</p>
<p>The government was divided into two parts. The "Jamahiriya sector" composed the General People's Congress, the General People's Committee, and the local <a href="/wiki/Basic_People%27s_Congress_(political)" title="Basic People's Congress (political)">Basic People's Congresses</a>. It was overseen by the "revolutionary sector," headed by Gaddafi and the surviving members of the RCC. They held office owing to their role in the 1969 coup—officially called "the Revolution"—and were therefore not subject to election. They oversaw the "revolutionary committees," which were nominally grass-roots organizations that helped keep the people engaged. As a result, although Gaddafi held no formal office after 1980, he retained absolute control of the government and the country<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_reforms">Administrative reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Administrative reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>All legislative and executive authority was vested in the GPC. This body, however, delegated most of its important authority to its general secretary and General Secretariat and to the General People's Committee. Gaddafi, as general secretary of the GPC, remained the primary decision maker, just as he had been when chairman of the RCC. In turn, all adults had the right and duty to participate in the deliberation of their local Basic People's Congress (BPC), whose decisions were passed up to the GPC for consideration and implementation as national policy. The BPCs were in theory the repository of ultimate political authority and decision making, embodying what Gaddafi termed direct "people's power". The 1977 declaration and its accompanying resolutions amounted to a fundamental revision of the 1969 constitutional proclamation, especially with respect to the structure and organization of the government at both national and subnational levels.</p>
<p>Continuing to revamp Libya's political and administrative structure, Gaddafi introduced yet another element into the body politic. Beginning in 1977, "revolutionary committees" were organized and assigned the task of "absolute revolutionary supervision of people's power"; that is, they were to guide the people's committees, "raise the general level of political consciousness and devotion to revolutionary ideals". In reality, the revolutionary committees were used to survey the population and repress any political opposition to Gaddafi's autocratic rule. Reportedly 10% to 20% of Libyans worked in surveillance for these committees, a proportion of informants on par with <a href="/wiki/Ba%27athist_Iraq" title="Ba'athist Iraq">Ba'athist Iraq</a> or <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gaddafiunrepentant_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddafiunrepentant-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Filled with politically astute zealots, the ubiquitous revolutionary committees in 1979 assumed control of BPC elections. Although they were not official government organs, the revolutionary committees became another mainstay of the domestic political scene. As with the people's committees and other administrative innovations since the revolution, the revolutionary committees fit the pattern of imposing a new element on the existing subnational system of government rather than eliminating or consolidating already existing structures. By the late 1970s, the result was an unnecessarily complex system of overlapping jurisdictions in which cooperation and coordination among different elements were compromised by ill-defined authority and responsibility.</p>
<p>The RCC was formally dissolved and the government was again reorganized into people's committees. A new General People's Committee (cabinet) was selected, each of its "secretaries" becoming head of a specialized people's committee; the exceptions were the "secretariats" of petroleum, foreign affairs, and heavy industry, where there were no people's committees. A proposal was also made to establish a "people's army" by substituting a national militia, being formed in the late 1970s, for the national army. Although the idea surfaced again in early 1982, it did not appear to be close to implementation.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_reforms">Economic reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Economic reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Libya" title="Economy of Libya">Economy of Libya</a></div>
<p>Remaking of the economy was parallel with the attempt to remold political and social institutions. Until the late 1970s, Libya's <a href="/wiki/Mixed_economy" title="Mixed economy">economy was mixed</a>, with a large role for private enterprise except in the fields of oil production and distribution, banking, and insurance. But according to volume two of Gaddafi's Green Book, which appeared in 1978, private retail trade, rent, and wages were forms of "exploitation" that should be abolished. Instead, <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_self-management" title="Workers' self-management">workers' self-management</a> committees and profit participation partnerships were to function in public and private enterprises.</p>
<p>A property law was passed that forbade ownership of more than one private dwelling, and Libyan workers took control of a large number of companies, turning them into state-run enterprises. Retail and wholesale trading operations were replaced by state-owned "people's supermarkets", where Libyans in theory could purchase whatever they needed at low prices. By 1981 the state had also restricted access to individual bank accounts to draw upon privately held funds for government projects.</p>
<p>Gaddafi's efforts also improved the average health of Libyans. In 2009, the CIA's <i>World Factbook</i> showed the average life expectancy of a Libyan to be 77 years (only one year less than that of an American citizen).</p>
<p>However, the measures created resentment and opposition among the newly dispossessed. The latter joined those already alienated, some of whom had begun to leave the country. By 1982, perhaps 50,000 to 100,000 Libyans had gone abroad; because many of the emigrants were among the enterprising and better educated Libyans, they represented a significant loss of managerial and technical expertise.</p>
<p>The government also built a trans-Sahara water pipeline from major aquifers to both a network of reservoirs and the towns of Tripoli, Sirte and Benghazi in 2006–2007, ending the city's water shortages, caused by the rising urban population.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> It is part of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Manmade_River" title="Great Manmade River" class="mw-redirect">Great Manmade River</a> project, started in 1984. It is pumping large resources of water from the <a href="/wiki/Nubian_Sandstone_Aquifer_System" title="Nubian Sandstone Aquifer System">Nubian Sandstone Aquifer System</a> to both urban populations and new irrigation projects around the country.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Libya continued to be plagued with a shortage of skilled labor, which had to be imported along with a broad range of consumer goods, both paid for with petroleum income. This same oil revenue, however, made possible a substantial improvement in the lives of virtually all Libyans. During the 1970s, the government succeeded in making major improvements in the general welfare of its citizens. By the 1980s Libyans enjoyed much improved housing and education, comprehensive social welfare services, and general standards of health that were among the highest in Africa.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wars_against_Chad_and_Egypt">Wars against Chad and Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Wars against Chad and Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chadian%E2%80%93Libyan_conflict" title="Chadian–Libyan conflict">Chadian–Libyan conflict</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toyota_War" title="Toyota War">Toyota War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Libyan%E2%80%93Egyptian_War" title="Libyan–Egyptian War">Libyan–Egyptian War</a></div>
<p>As early as 1969, Gaddafi waged a campaign against <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a>. Scholar Gerard Prunier claims Part of his hostility was apparently because <a href="/wiki/President_of_Chad" title="President of Chad" class="mw-redirect">Chadian President</a> <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Tombalbaye" title="François Tombalbaye">François Tombalbaye</a> was Christian.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> Libya was also involved in a sometimes violent territorial dispute with neighbouring Chad over the <a href="/wiki/Aouzou_Strip" title="Aouzou Strip">Aouzou Strip</a>, which Libya occupied in 1973. This dispute eventually led to the <a href="/wiki/Chadian-Libyan_conflict" title="Chadian-Libyan conflict" class="mw-redirect">Libyan invasion</a> of Chad. The prolonged foray of Libyan troops into the <a href="/wiki/Aozou_Strip" title="Aozou Strip" class="mw-redirect">Aozou Strip</a> in northern Chad, was finally repulsed in 1987, when extensive US and French help to Chadian rebel forces and the government headed by former Defence Minister <a href="/wiki/Hissein_Habr%C3%A9" title="Hissein Habré" class="mw-redirect">Hissein Habré</a> finally led to a Chadian victory in the so-called <a href="/wiki/Toyota_War" title="Toyota War">Toyota War</a>. The conflict ended in a ceasefire in 1987. After a judgement of the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a> on 13 February 1994, Libya withdrew troops from Chad the same year and the dispute was settled.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1977, Gaddafi dispatched his military across the border to Egypt, but Egyptian forces fought back in the <a href="/wiki/Libyan%E2%80%93Egyptian_War" title="Libyan–Egyptian War">Libyan–Egyptian War</a>. Both nations agreed to a ceasefire under the mediation of the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Algeria" title="President of Algeria">President of Algeria</a> <a href="/wiki/Houari_Boumedi%C3%A8ne" title="Houari Boumediène" class="mw-redirect">Houari Boumediène</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_Legion">Islamic Legion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Islamic Legion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Legion" title="Islamic Legion">Islamic Legion</a></div>
<p>In 1972, Gaddafi created the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Legion" title="Islamic Legion">Islamic Legion</a> as a tool to unify and Arabize the region. The priority of the Legion was first Chad, and then Sudan. In <a href="/wiki/Darfur" title="Darfur">Darfur</a>, a western province of Sudan, Gaddafi supported the creation of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Arab_Gathering&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Arab Gathering (page does not exist)">Arab Gathering</a> (Tajammu al-Arabi), which according to Gérard Prunier was "a militantly racist and pan-Arabist organization which stressed the 'Arab' character of the province."<sup id="cite_ref-prunier45_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prunier45-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> The two organizations shared members and a source of support, and the distinction between them is often ambiguous.</p>
<p>This Islamic Legion was mostly composed of immigrants from poorer <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahelian</a> countries,<sup id="cite_ref-Nolutshungu220_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nolutshungu220-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> but also, according to a source, thousands of Pakistanis who had been recruited in 1981 with the false promise of civilian jobs once in Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> Generally speaking, the Legion's members were immigrants who had gone to Libya with no thought of fighting wars, and had been provided with inadequate military training and had sparse commitment. A French journalist, speaking of the Legion's forces in Chad, observed that they were "foreigners, Arabs or Africans, <a href="/wiki/Mercenary" title="Mercenary">mercenaries</a> in spite of themselves, wretches who had come to Libya hoping for a civilian job, but found themselves signed up more or less by force to go and fight in an unknown desert."<sup id="cite_ref-Nolutshungu220_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nolutshungu220-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At the beginning of the 1987 Libyan offensive in Chad, it maintained a force of 2,000 in Darfur. The nearly continuous cross-border raids that resulted greatly contributed to a separate ethnic conflict within Darfur that killed about 9,000 people between 1985 and 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Janjaweed" title="Janjaweed">Janjaweed</a>, a group accused by the US of <a href="/wiki/Darfur_genocide" title="Darfur genocide" class="mw-redirect">carrying out a genocide in Darfur in the 2000s</a>, emerged in 1988 and some of its leaders are former legionnaires.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Attempts_at_nuclear_and_chemical_weapons">Attempts at nuclear and chemical weapons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Attempts at nuclear and chemical weapons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Libya_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="Libya and weapons of mass destruction">Libya and weapons of mass destruction</a></div>
<p>In 1972, Gaddafi tried to buy a nuclear bomb from the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China" class="mw-redirect">People's Republic of China</a>. He then tried to get a bomb from Pakistan, but Pakistan severed its ties before it succeeded in building a bomb.<sup id="cite_ref-riskreport_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-riskreport-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1978, Gaddafi turned to Pakistan's rival, India, for help building its own nuclear bomb.<sup id="cite_ref-GlobalSecurity.org_and_John_E._Pike_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GlobalSecurity.org_and_John_E._Pike-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> In July 1978, Libya and India signed a <a href="/wiki/Memorandum_of_understanding" title="Memorandum of understanding">memorandum of understanding</a> to cooperate in peaceful applications of nuclear energy as part of India's Atom of Peace policy.<sup id="cite_ref-GlobalSecurity.org_and_John_E._Pike_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GlobalSecurity.org_and_John_E._Pike-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1991, then <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Pakistan" title="Prime Minister of Pakistan">Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Navaz_Sharif" title="Navaz Sharif" class="mw-redirect">Navaz Sharif</a> paid a <a href="/wiki/State_visit" title="State visit">state visit</a> to Libya to hold talks on the promotion of a <a href="/wiki/Free_trade_agreement" title="Free trade agreement" class="mw-redirect">Free Trade Agreement</a> between Pakistan and Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-TahirKhalil_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TahirKhalil-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> However, Gaddafi focused on demanding Pakistan's Prime Minister sell him a nuclear weapon, which surprised many of the Prime Minister's delegation members and journalists.<sup id="cite_ref-TahirKhalil_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TahirKhalil-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> When Prime minister Sharif refused Gaddafi's demand, Gaddafi disrespected him, calling him a "Corrupt politician", a term which insulted and surprised Sharif.<sup id="cite_ref-TahirKhalil_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TahirKhalil-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> The Prime minister cancelled the talks, returned to Pakistan and expelled the Libyan Ambassador from Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-TahirKhalil_37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TahirKhalil-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Thailand reported its citizens had helped build storage facilities for nerve gas.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> Germany sentenced a businessman, Jurgen Hippenstiel-Imhausen, to five years in prison for involvement in Libyan chemical weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-riskreport_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-riskreport-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> Inspectors from the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_Weapons_Convention" title="Chemical Weapons Convention">Chemical Weapons Convention</a> (CWC) verified in 2004 that Libya owned a stockpile of 23 metric tons of <a href="/wiki/Mustard_gas" title="Mustard gas" class="mw-redirect">mustard gas</a> and more than 1,300 metric tons of precursor chemicals.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gulf_of_Sidra_incidents_and_US_air_strikes">Gulf of Sidra incidents and US air strikes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Gulf of Sidra incidents and US air strikes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Sidra_incident_(1981)" title="Gulf of Sidra incident (1981)">Gulf of Sidra incident (1981)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Action_in_the_Gulf_of_Sidra_(1986)" title="Action in the Gulf of Sidra (1986)">Action in the Gulf of Sidra (1986)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Libya_(1986)" title="Bombing of Libya (1986)" class="mw-redirect">Bombing of Libya (1986)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Sidra_incident_(1989)" title="Gulf of Sidra incident (1989)">Gulf of Sidra incident (1989)</a></div>
<p>When Libya was under pressure from international disputes, on 19 August 1981, a naval <a href="/wiki/Dogfight" title="Dogfight">dogfight</a> occurred over the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Sidra" title="Gulf of Sidra">Gulf of Sidra</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>. US <a href="/wiki/F-14_Tomcat" title="F-14 Tomcat" class="mw-redirect">F-14 Tomcat</a> jets fired anti-aircraft missiles against a formation of Libyan fighter jets in this dogfight and shot down two <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libyan</a> <a href="/wiki/Sukhoi_Su-17" title="Sukhoi Su-17">Su-22 Fitter</a> attack aircraft. This naval action was a result of claiming the territory and losses from the previous incident. A second dogfight occurred on 4 January 1989; US carrier-based jets also shot down two <a href="/wiki/Libyan_Air_Force_(1951%E2%80%932011)" title="Libyan Air Force (1951–2011)" class="mw-redirect">Libyan</a> <a href="/wiki/Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-23" title="Mikoyan-Gurevich MiG-23">MiG-23 Flogger-Es</a> in the same place, adding up to a disastrous loss of Libya's air force.</p>
<p>A similar action occurred on 23 March 1986; while patrolling the Gulf, US naval forces attacked a sizable naval force and various SAM sites defending Libyan territory. US fighter jets and fighter-bombers destroyed SAM launching facilities and sank various naval vessels, killing 35 seamen. This was a reprisal for terrorist hijackings between June and December 1985.</p>
<p>On 5 April 1986, Libyan agents <a href="/wiki/1986_Berlin_discotheque_bombing" title="1986 Berlin discotheque bombing">bombed "La Belle" nightclub in West Berlin</a>, killing three and injuring 229. Gaddafi's plan was intercepted by several national intelligence agencies and more detailed information was retrieved four years later from <a href="/wiki/Stasi" title="Stasi">Stasi</a> archives. The Libyan agents who had carried out the operation, from the Libyan embassy in <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a>, were prosecuted by the reunited Germany in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In response to the discotheque bombing, joint US Air Force, Navy and Marine Corps air-strikes took place against Libya on 15 April 1986 and code-named Operation El Dorado Canyon and known as the <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Libya_(1986)" title="Bombing of Libya (1986)" class="mw-redirect">1986 bombing of Libya</a>. Air defenses, three army bases, and two airfields in <a href="/wiki/Tripoli" title="Tripoli">Tripoli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benghazi" title="Benghazi">Benghazi</a> were bombed. The surgical strikes failed to kill Gaddafi but he lost a few dozen military officers. Gaddafi spread propaganda how it had killed his "adopted daughter" and how victims had been all "civilians". Despite the variations of the stories, the campaign was successful, and a large proportion of the Western press reported the government's stories as facts.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Following the 1986 bombing of Libya, Gaddafi intensified his support for anti-American government organizations. He financed <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Fort" title="Jeff Fort">Jeff Forts</a> <a href="/wiki/Al-Rukn" title="Al-Rukn" class="mw-redirect">Al-Rukn</a> faction of the Chicago <a href="/wiki/Black_P._Stones" title="Black P. Stones" class="mw-redirect">Black P. Stones</a> gang, in their emergence as an indigenous anti-American armed revolutionary movement.<sup id="cite_ref-Bodansky_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bodansky-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> Al-Rukn members were arrested in 1986 for preparing strikes on behalf of Libya, including blowing up US government buildings and bringing down an airplane; the Al-Rukn defendants were convicted in 1987 of "offering to commit bombings and assassinations on US soil for Libyan payment."<sup id="cite_ref-Bodansky_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bodansky-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1986, Libyan state television announced that Libya was training suicide squads to attack American and European interests. He began financing the IRA again in 1986, to retaliate against the British for harboring American fighter planes.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi announced that he had won a spectacular military victory over the US and the country was officially renamed the "Great Socialist People's Libyan Arab Jamahiriyah".<sup id="cite_ref-Brian.252520Lee.252520Davis.252520183_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brian.252520Lee.252520Davis.252520183-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> However, his speech appeared devoid of passion and even the "victory" celebrations appeared unusual. Criticism of Gaddafi by ordinary Libyan citizens became more bold, such as defacing of Gaddafi posters.<sup id="cite_ref-Brian.252520Lee.252520Davis.252520183_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brian.252520Lee.252520Davis.252520183-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> The raids against Libyan military had brought the government to its weakest point in 17 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Brian.252520Lee.252520Davis.252520183_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brian.252520Lee.252520Davis.252520183-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_relations">International relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: International relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Foreign relations of Libya under Muammar Gaddafi">Foreign relations of Libya under Muammar Gaddafi</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Uganda%E2%80%93Tanzania_War" title="Uganda–Tanzania War">Uganda–Tanzania War</a></div>
<p>Gaddafi was a close supporter of Ugandan President <a href="/wiki/Idi_Amin" title="Idi Amin">Idi Amin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi sent thousands of troops to fight against Tanzania on behalf of Idi Amin. About 600 Libyan soldiers lost their lives attempting to defend the collapsing presidency of Amin. Amin exiled from Uganda to Libya before settling in Saudi Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-stanik23_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanik23-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi also aided <a href="/wiki/Jean-B%C3%A9del_Bokassa" title="Jean-Bédel Bokassa">Jean-Bédel Bokassa</a>, the Emperor of the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Empire" title="Central African Empire">Central African Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-stanik23_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanik23-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-leedavis16_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leedavis16-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> He also intervened militarily in the renewed Central African Republic in 2001 to protect his ally <a href="/wiki/Ange-F%C3%A9lix_Patass%C3%A9" title="Ange-Félix Patassé">Ange-Félix Patassé</a>. Patassé signed a deal giving Libya a 99-year lease to exploit all of that country's natural resources, including uranium, copper, diamonds, and oil.<sup id="cite_ref-Harvard.252520for.252520Tyrants_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvard.252520for.252520Tyrants-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi supported Soviet protégé <a href="/wiki/Haile_Mariam_Mengistu" title="Haile Mariam Mengistu" class="mw-redirect">Haile Mariam Mengistu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-leedavis16_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leedavis16-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi opposed <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> and forged a good relationship with <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a>, who named his grandson after Gaddafi and called Gaddafi one of the 20th century’s greatest freedom fighters, and insisted the eventual collapse of the apartheid system owed much to Gaddafi and Libyan support.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> In turn, Mandela later played a key role in helping Gaddafi gain mainstream acceptance in the Western world later in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_africa_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_africa-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mandela_speech_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mandela_speech-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> Over the years, Gaddafi came to be seen as a hero in much of Africa due to his revolutionary image.<sup id="cite_ref-dailytimes_ng_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dailytimes_ng-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi was a strong supporter of <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwean</a> President <a href="/wiki/Robert_Mugabe" title="Robert Mugabe">Robert Mugabe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi's World Revolutionary Center (WRC) near Benghazi become a training center for groups backed by Gaddafi.<sup id="cite_ref-Harvard.252520for.252520Tyrants_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvard.252520for.252520Tyrants-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> Graduates in power as of 2011 include <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Compaor%C3%A9" title="Blaise Compaoré">Blaise Compaoré</a> of Burkina Faso and <a href="/wiki/Idriss_D%C3%A9by" title="Idriss Déby">Idriss Déby</a> of Chad.<sup id="cite_ref-school.252520for.252520scoundrels_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-school.252520for.252520scoundrels-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi trained and supported Liberian warlord-president <a href="/wiki/Charles_Taylor_(Liberia)" title="Charles Taylor (Liberia)" class="mw-redirect">Charles Taylor</a>, who was indicted by the <a href="/wiki/Special_Court_for_Sierra_Leone" title="Special Court for Sierra Leone">Special Court for Sierra Leone</a> for war crimes and <a href="/wiki/Crimes_against_humanity" title="Crimes against humanity">crimes against humanity</a> committed during the conflict in Sierra Leone.<sup id="cite_ref-economistfall_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economistfall-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Foday_Sankoh" title="Foday Sankoh">Foday Sankoh</a>, the founder of <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_United_Front" title="Revolutionary United Front">Revolutionary United Front</a>, was also Gaddafi's graduate. According to <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Farah" title="Douglas Farah">Douglas Farah</a>, "The amputation of the arms and legs of men, women, and children as part of a scorched-earth campaign was designed to take over the region's rich diamond fields and was backed by Gaddafi, who routinely reviewed their progress and supplied weapons".<sup id="cite_ref-school.252520for.252520scoundrels_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-school.252520for.252520scoundrels-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi's strong military support and finances gained him allies across the continent. He had himself crowned with the title "<i>King of Kings of Africa</i>" in 2008, in the presence of over 200 African traditional rulers and kings, although his views on African political and military unification received a lukewarm response from their governments.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc7588033_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc7588033-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> His 2009 forum for African kings was canceled by the Ugandan hosts, who believed that traditional rulers discussing politics would lead to instability.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> On 1&#160;February&#160;2009, a '<a href="/wiki/Coronation" title="Coronation">coronation</a> ceremony' in <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa" title="Addis Ababa">Addis Ababa</a>, Ethiopia, was held to coincide with the 53rd African Union Summit, at which he was elected head of the African Union for the year.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi told the assembled African leaders: "I shall continue to insist that our sovereign countries work to achieve the <a href="/wiki/United_States_of_Africa" title="United States of Africa">United States of Africa</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1986, 2000, and the months prior to the 2011 civil war, Gaddafi announced plans for a unified African <a href="/wiki/Gold_dinar" title="Gold dinar">gold dinar</a> currency, to challenge the dominance of the US$ and Euro currencies. The African dinar would have been measured directly in terms of gold.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaddafi_and_international_terrorism">Gaddafi and international terrorism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Gaddafi and international terrorism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Active_measures" title="Active measures">Active measures</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;position:relative;display:block;width:400px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_1&quot;
