<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oil_Rich_Libya.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1972 <a href="/wiki/Newsreel" title="Newsreel">newsreel</a> including interview with Gaddafi about his support for radical groups</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1971 Gaddafi warned that if France opposes Libyan military occupation of Chad, he will use all weapons in the war against France including the "revolutionary weapon".<sup id="cite_ref-Brian.252520Lee.252520Davis.252520183_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brian.252520Lee.252520Davis.252520183-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> On 11 June 1972, Gaddafi announced that any Arab wishing to volunteer for Palestinian terrorist groups "can register his name at any Libyan embassy will be given adequate training for combat". He also promised financial support for attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-leedavis182_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leedavis182-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> On 7 October 1972, Gaddafi praised the <a href="/wiki/Lod_Airport_massacre" title="Lod Airport massacre">Lod Airport massacre</a>, executed by the communist <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Red_Army" title="Japanese Red Army">Japanese Red Army</a>, and demanded Palestinian terrorist groups to carry out similar attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-leedavis182_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leedavis182-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Reportedly, Gaddafi was a major financier of the "<a href="/wiki/Black_September_(group)" title="Black September (group)" class="mw-redirect">Black September</a> Movement" which perpetrated the <a href="/wiki/Munich_massacre" title="Munich massacre">Munich massacre</a> at the <a href="/wiki/1972_Summer_Olympics" title="1972 Summer Olympics">1972 Summer Olympics</a>.<a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.ynetnews.com/articles/0,7340,L-4033566,00.html">[2]</a> In 1973 the <a href="/wiki/Naval_Service_(Ireland)" title="Naval Service (Ireland)">Irish Naval Service</a> intercepted the vessel <i>Claudia</i> in Irish territorial waters, which carried Soviet arms from Libya to the Provisional IRA.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1976 after a series of terror activities by the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_IRA" title="Provisional IRA" class="mw-redirect">Provisional IRA</a>, Gaddafi announced that "the bombs which are convulsing Britain and breaking its spirit are the bombs of Libyan people. We have sent them to the Irish revolutionaries so that the British will pay the price for their past deeds".<sup id="cite_ref-leedavis182_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leedavis182-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the Philippines, Libya backed the <a href="/wiki/Moro_Islamic_Liberation_Front" title="Moro Islamic Liberation Front">Moro Islamic Liberation Front</a>, which continues to carry out acts of violence in an effort to establish a separatist Islamic state in the southern Philippines.<sup id="cite_ref-Geoffrey.252520Leslie.252520Simons.252520281_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geoffrey.252520Leslie.252520Simons.252520281-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> Libya has also supported the <a href="/wiki/New_People%27s_Army" title="New People's Army">New People's Army</a><sup id="cite_ref-A.252520Rogue.252520Returns_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A.252520Rogue.252520Returns-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> and Libyan agents were seen meeting with the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_the_Philippines" title="Communist Party of the Philippines">Communist Party of the Philippines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> Islamist terrorist group <a href="/wiki/Abu_Sayyaf" title="Abu Sayyaf">Abu Sayyaf</a> has also been suspected of receiving Libyan funding.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2002_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2002-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi also became a strong supporter of the <a href="/wiki/Palestine_Liberation_Organization" title="Palestine Liberation Organization">Palestine Liberation Organization</a>, which support ultimately harmed Libya's relations with Egypt, when in 1979 Egypt pursued a peace agreement with Israel. As Libya's relations with Egypt worsened, Gaddafi sought closer relations with the Soviet Union. Libya became the first country outside the Soviet bloc to receive the supersonic <a href="/wiki/Mikoyan-Gurevich_MiG-25" title="Mikoyan-Gurevich MiG-25">MiG-25</a> combat fighters, but Soviet-Libyan relations remained relatively distant. Gaddafi also sought to increase Libyan influence, especially in states with an <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> population, by calling for the creation of a Saharan Islamic state and supporting anti-government forces in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>.</p>
<p>In the 1970s and the 1980s, this support was sometimes so freely given that even the most unsympathetic groups could obtain Libyan support; often the groups represented ideologies far removed from Gaddafi's own. Gaddafi's approach often tended to confuse international opinion.</p>
<p>In October 1981 Egypt's President <a href="/wiki/Anwar_Sadat" title="Anwar Sadat">Anwar Sadat</a> was assassinated. Gaddafi applauded the murder and remarked that it was a "punishment".<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In December 1981, the <a href="/wiki/US_State_Department" title="US State Department" class="mw-redirect">US State Department</a> invalidated US passports for travel to Libya, and in March 1982, the U.S. declared a ban on the import of Libyan <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi reportedly spent hundreds of millions of the government's money on training and arming Sandinistas in Nicaragua.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Ortega" title="Daniel Ortega">Daniel Ortega</a>, the President of Nicaragua, was his ally.</p>
<p>In April 1984, Libyan refugees in London protested against execution of two dissidents. Communications intercepted by MI5 show that Tripoli ordered its diplomats to direct violence against the demonstrators. Libyan diplomats shot at 11 people and killed British policewoman <a href="/wiki/Yvonne_Fletcher" title="Yvonne Fletcher" class="mw-redirect">Yvonne Fletcher</a>. The incident led to the breaking off of <a href="/wiki/Diplomacy" title="Diplomacy">diplomatic relations</a> between the United Kingdom and Libya for over a decade.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After December 1985 <a href="/wiki/Rome_and_Vienna_airport_attacks" title="Rome and Vienna airport attacks">Rome and Vienna airport attacks</a>, which killed 19 and wounded around 140, Gaddafi indicated that he would continue to support the <a href="/wiki/Red_Army_Faction" title="Red Army Faction">Red Army Faction</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Red_Brigades" title="Red Brigades">Red Brigades</a>, and the Irish Republican Army as long as European countries support anti-Gaddafi Libyans.<sup id="cite_ref-St.25252E.252520John_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-St.25252E.252520John-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> The Foreign Minister of Libya also called the massacres "heroic acts".<sup id="cite_ref-Seale245_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seale245-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1986, Libyan state television announced that Libya was training suicide squads to attack American and European interests.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On 5 April 1986, Libyan agents were alleged with <a href="/wiki/1986_Berlin_discotheque_bombing" title="1986 Berlin discotheque bombing">bombing the "La Belle" nightclub in West Berlin</a>, killing three people and injuring 229 people who were spending evening there. Gaddafi's plan was intercepted by Western intelligence. More-detailed information was retrieved years later when <a href="/wiki/Stasi" title="Stasi">Stasi</a> archives were investigated by the reunited Germany. Libyan agents who had carried out the operation from the Libyan embassy in East Germany were prosecuted by reunited Germany in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In May 1987, Australia broke off relations with Libya because of its role in fueling violence in Oceania.<sup id="cite_ref-A.252520Rogue.252520Returns_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A.252520Rogue.252520Returns-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated758_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated758-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The.252520Australian.25252024.252520February.2525202011_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The.252520Australian.25252024.252520February.2525202011-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Under Gaddafi, Libya had a long history of supporting the <a href="/wiki/List_of_organisations_known_as_the_Irish_Republican_Army" title="List of organisations known as the Irish Republican Army" class="mw-redirect">Irish Republican Army</a>. In late 1987 French authorities stopped a merchant vessel, the <i>MV Eksund</i>, which was delivering a 150-ton Libyan arms shipment to the IRA.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> In Britain, Gaddafi's best-known political subsidiary is the <a href="/wiki/Workers_Revolutionary_Party_(UK)" title="Workers Revolutionary Party (UK)">Workers Revolutionary Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-The.252520Australian.25252024.252520February.2525202011_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The.252520Australian.25252024.252520February.2525202011-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi fueled a number of Islamist and communist groups in the Philippines, including the <a href="/wiki/New_People%27s_Army" title="New People's Army">New People's Army</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_the_Philippines" title="Communist Party of the Philippines">Communist Party of the Philippines</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Moro_Islamic_Liberation_Front" title="Moro Islamic Liberation Front">Moro Islamic Liberation Front</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gaddafiunrepentant_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddafiunrepentant-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Geoffrey.252520Leslie.252520Simons.252520281_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geoffrey.252520Leslie.252520Simons.252520281-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-A.252520Rogue.252520Returns_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A.252520Rogue.252520Returns-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2002_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2002-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-St.25252E.252520John_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-St.25252E.252520John-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In Indonesia, the <a href="/wiki/Free_Aceh_Movement" title="Free Aceh Movement">Free Aceh Movement</a> was a Libyan-backed militant group.<sup id="cite_ref-Aceh_chronlogy_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aceh_chronlogy-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vanuatu" title="Vanuatu">Vanuatu</a>'s ruling party enjoyed Libyan support.<sup id="cite_ref-A.252520Rogue.252520Returns_70-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A.252520Rogue.252520Returns-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In New Zealand, Libya attempted to radicalize <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_people" title="Māori people">Māoris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-A.252520Rogue.252520Returns_70-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A.252520Rogue.252520Returns-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In Australia, there were several cases of attempted radicalisation of Australian Aborigines, with individuals receiving paramilitary training in Libya. Libya put several left-wing unions on the Libyan payroll, such as the Food Preservers Union (FPU) and the Federated Confectioners Association of Australia (FCA). Labour Party politician <a href="/wiki/Bill_Hartley_(activist)" title="Bill Hartley (activist)">Bill Hartley</a>, the secretary of Libya-Australia friendship society, was long-term supporter of Gaddafi and Saddam Hussein.<sup id="cite_ref-A.252520Rogue.252520Returns_70-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A.252520Rogue.252520Returns-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated758_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated758-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The.252520Australian.25252024.252520February.2525202011_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The.252520Australian.25252024.252520February.2525202011-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1980s, the Libyan government purchased advertisements in Arabic-language newspapers in Australia asking for Australian Arabs to join the military units of his worldwide struggle against imperialism. In part,because of this, Australia banned recruitment of foreign mercenaries in Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-The.252520Australian.25252024.252520February.2525202011_82-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The.252520Australian.25252024.252520February.2525202011-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi developed a relationship with the <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Armed_Forces_of_Colombia" title="Revolutionary Armed Forces of Colombia" class="mw-redirect">Revolutionary Armed Forces of Colombia</a>, becoming acquainted with its leaders in meetings of revolutionary groups regularly hosted in Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-Harvard.252520for.252520Tyrants_49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvard.252520for.252520Tyrants-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-school.252520for.252520scoundrels_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-school.252520for.252520scoundrels-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Some publications were financed by Gaddafi. The Socialist Labour League's <i>Workers News</i> was one such publication: "in among the routine denunciations of uranium mining and calls for greater trade union militancy would be a couple of pages extolling Gaddafi's fatuous and incoherent green book and the Libyan revolution."<sup id="cite_ref-The.252520Australian.25252024.252520February.2525202011_82-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The.252520Australian.25252024.252520February.2525202011-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="International_sanctions_after_the_Lockerbie_bombing_.281992.E2.80.932003.29">International sanctions after the Lockerbie bombing (1992–2003)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: International sanctions after the Lockerbie bombing (1992–2003)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_731" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 731">United Nations Security Council Resolution 731</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_748" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 748">United Nations Security Council Resolution 748</a></div>
<p>Libya was accused in the 1988 bombing of <a href="/wiki/Pan_Am_flight_103" title="Pan Am flight 103" class="mw-redirect">Pan Am flight 103</a> over <a href="/wiki/Lockerbie" title="Lockerbie">Lockerbie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>; UN sanctions were imposed in 1992. <a href="/wiki/UN_Security_Council" title="UN Security Council" class="mw-redirect">UN Security Council</a> resolutions (UNSCRs) passed in 1992 and 1993 obliged Libya to fulfill requirements related to the Pan Am 103 bombing before sanctions could be lifted, leading to Libya's political and economic isolation for most of the 1990s. The UN sanctions cut airline connections with the outer world, reduced diplomatic representation and prohibited the sale of military equipment. Oil-related sanctions were assessed by some as equally significant for their exceptions: thus sanctions froze Libya's foreign assets (but excluded revenue from oil and natural gas and agricultural commodities) and banned the sale to Libya of refinery or pipeline equipment (but excluded oil <i>production</i> equipment).</p>
<p>Under the sanctions Libya's refining capacity eroded. Libya's role on the international stage grew less provocative after UN sanctions were imposed. In 1999, Libya fulfilled one of the UNSCR requirements by surrendering two Libyans suspected in connection with the bombing for trial before a Scottish court in the Netherlands. One of these suspects, Abdel Basset al-Megrahi, was found guilty; the other was acquitted. UN sanctions against Libya were subsequently suspended. The full lifting of the sanctions, contingent on Libya's compliance with the remaining UNSCRs, including acceptance of responsibility for the actions of its officials and payment of appropriate compensation, was passed 12 September 2003, explicitly linked to the release of up to $2.7 billion in Libyan funds to the families of the 1988 attack's 270 victims.</p>
<p>In 2002, Gaddafi paid a ransom reportedly worth tens of millions of dollars to <a href="/wiki/Abu_Sayyaf" title="Abu Sayyaf">Abu Sayyaf</a>, a Filipino Islamist militancy, to release a number of kidnapped tourists. He presented it as an act of goodwill to Western countries; nevertheless the money helped the group to expand its operation.<sup id="cite_ref-Gaddafiunrepentant_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddafiunrepentant-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Normalization_of_international_relations_.282003.E2.80.932010.29">Normalization of international relations (2003–2010)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Normalization of international relations (2003–2010)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In December 2003, Libya announced that it had agreed to reveal and end its programs to develop weapons of mass destruction and to renounce terrorism, and Gaddafi made significant strides in normalizing relations with western nations. He received various Western European leaders as well as many working-level and commercial delegations, and made his first trip to Western Europe in 15 years when he traveled to <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a> in April 2004. Libya responded in good faith to legal cases brought against it in U.S. courts for terrorist acts that predate its renunciation of violence. Claims for compensation in the Lockerbie bombing, LaBelle disco bombing, and UTA 772 bombing cases are ongoing. The U.S. rescinded Libya's designation as a <a href="/wiki/State_Sponsors_of_Terrorism" title="State Sponsors of Terrorism">state sponsor of terrorism</a> in June 2006. In late 2007, Libya was elected by the General Assembly to a nonpermanent seat on the United Nations Security Council for the 2008–2009 term. Currently, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Enduring_Freedom_-_Trans_Sahara" title="Operation Enduring Freedom - Trans Sahara" class="mw-redirect">Operation Enduring Freedom - Trans Sahara</a> is being fought in Libya's portion of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara_Desert" title="Sahara Desert" class="mw-redirect">Sahara Desert</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Purification_laws">Purification laws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Purification laws">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_Libya" title="LGBT rights in Libya">LGBT rights in Libya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Libya" title="Women in Libya">Women in Libya</a></div>
<p>In 1994, the <a href="/wiki/General_People%27s_Congress_(Libya)" title="General People's Congress (Libya)">General People's Congress</a> approved the introduction of "purification laws" to be put into effect, punishing theft by the amputation of limbs, and fornication and adultery by flogging.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Under the Libyan constitution, homosexual relations are punishable by up to five years in jail.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition.2C_coups_and_revolts">Opposition, coups and revolts <span id="Opposition"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Opposition, coups and revolts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/National_Front_for_the_Salvation_of_Libya" title="National Front for the Salvation of Libya">National Front for the Salvation of Libya</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Conference_for_the_Libyan_Opposition" title="National Conference for the Libyan Opposition">National Conference for the Libyan Opposition</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anti-Gaddafi_forces" title="Anti-Gaddafi forces">Anti-Gaddafi forces</a></div>
<p>Throughout his long rule, Gaddafi had to defend his position against opposition and coup attempts, emerging both from the military and from the general population. He reacted to these threats on one hand by maintaining a careful balance of power between the forces in the country, and by brutal repression on the other. Gaddafi successfully balanced the various <a href="/wiki/Tribes_of_Libya" title="Tribes of Libya" class="mw-redirect">tribes of Libya</a> one against the other by distributing his favours. To forestall a military coup, he deliberately weakened the <a href="/wiki/Armed_forces_of_the_Libyan_Arab_Jamahiriya" title="Armed forces of the Libyan Arab Jamahiriya" class="mw-redirect">Libyan Armed Forces</a> by regularly rotating officers, relying instead on loyal elite troops such as his <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Guard_Corps" title="Revolutionary Guard Corps">Revolutionary Guard Corps</a>, the special-forces <a href="/wiki/Khamis_Brigade" title="Khamis Brigade">Khamis Brigade</a> and his personal <a href="/wiki/Amazonian_Guard" title="Amazonian Guard">Amazonian Guard</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_repression_and_.22Green_Terror.22">Political repression and "Green Terror"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Political repression and &quot;Green Terror&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Libya" title="Human rights in Libya">Human rights in Libya</a></div>
<p>The term "Green Terror" is used to describe campaigns of violence and intimidation against opponents of Gaddafi, particularly in reference to wave of oppression during Libya's <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution_(Libya)" title="Cultural Revolution (Libya)">cultural revolution</a>, or to the wave of highly publicized hangings of regime opponents that began with the <a href="/wiki/Execution_of_Al-Sadek_Hamed_Al-Shuwehdy" title="Execution of Al-Sadek Hamed Al-Shuwehdy">Execution of Al-Sadek Hamed Al-Shuwehdy</a>.</p>
<p>Dissent was illegal under Law 75 of 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-Gaddafiunrepentant_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddafiunrepentant-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Reportedly 10 to 20 percent of Libyans worked in surveillance for Gaddafi's Revolutionary Committees<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, a proportion of informants on par with <a href="/wiki/Saddam_Hussein" title="Saddam Hussein">Saddam Hussein</a>'s Iraq or <a href="/wiki/Kim_Jong_Il" title="Kim Jong Il" class="mw-redirect">Kim Jong Il</a>'s North Korea. The surveillance took place in government, in factories, and in the education sector.<sup id="cite_ref-Gaddafiunrepentant_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddafiunrepentant-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Following an abortive attempt to replace English foreign language education with Russian,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> in recent years English has been taught in Libyan schools from a primary level, and students have access to English-language media.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> However, one protester in 2011 described the situation as: "None of us can speak English or French. He kept us ignorant and blindfolded".<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> Prisons are run with little or no documentation of the inmate population or of such basic data as prisoner's crime and sentence.<sup id="cite_ref-Gaddafiunrepentant_24-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddafiunrepentant-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>According to the 2009 <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_Press_(report)" title="Freedom of the Press (report)">Freedom of the Press Index</a>, Libya is the most censored country in the Middle East and North Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition_to_the_Jamahiriya_reforms">Opposition to the Jamahiriya reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Opposition to the Jamahiriya reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the late 1970s, some exiled Libyans<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (November 2011)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> formed active opposition groups. In early 1979, Gaddafi warned opposition leaders to return home immediately or face "liquidation." When caught, they could face being sentenced and hanged in public.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is the Libyan people's responsibility to liquidate such scums who are distorting Libya's image abroad.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— Gaddafi talking about exiles in 1982.<sup id="cite_ref-Brian.252520Lee.252520Davis.252520183_45-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brian.252520Lee.252520Davis.252520183-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Gaddafi employed his network of diplomats and recruits to assassinate dozens of his critics around the world. <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a> listed at least twenty-five assassinations between 1980 and 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-Gaddafiunrepentant_24-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddafiunrepentant-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated758_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated758-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi's agents were active in the U.K., where many Libyans had sought asylum. After Libyan diplomats shot at 15 anti-Gaddafi protesters from inside the Libyan embassy's first floor and killed <a href="/wiki/Yvonne_Fletcher" title="Yvonne Fletcher" class="mw-redirect">a British policewoman</a>, the U.K. broke off relations with Gaddafi's government as a result of the incident.</p>
<p>Even the U.S. could not protect dissidents from Libya. In 1980, a Libyan agent attempted to assassinate dissident <a href="/w/index.php?title=Faisal_Zagallai&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Faisal Zagallai (page does not exist)">Faisal Zagallai</a>, a doctoral student at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Colorado_at_Boulder" title="University of Colorado at Boulder" class="mw-redirect">University of Colorado at Boulder</a>. The bullets left Zagallai partially blinded.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> A defector was kidnapped and executed in 1990 just before he was about to receive U.S. citizenship.<sup id="cite_ref-Gaddafiunrepentant_24-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaddafiunrepentant-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi asserted in June 1984 that killings could be carried out even when the dissidents were on pilgrimage in the holy city of <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>. In August 1984, one Libyan plot was thwarted in Mecca.<sup id="cite_ref-Brian.252520Lee.252520Davis.252520183_45-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brian.252520Lee.252520Davis.252520183-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2004, Libya still provided bounties for heads of critics, including 1&#160;million dollars for <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ashur_Shamis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ashur Shamis (page does not exist)">Ashur Shamis</a>, a Libyan-British journalist.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>There is indication that between the years of 2002 and 2007, Libya's Gaddafi-era <a href="/wiki/Mukhabarat_el-Jamahiriya" title="Mukhabarat el-Jamahiriya">intelligence service</a> had a partnership with western spy organizations including <a href="/wiki/MI6" title="MI6" class="mw-redirect">MI6</a> and the <a href="/wiki/CIA" title="CIA" class="mw-redirect">CIA</a>, who voluntarily provided information on Libyan dissidents in the United States and Canada in exchange for using Libya as a base for <a href="/wiki/Extraordinary_renditions" title="Extraordinary renditions" class="mw-redirect">extraordinary renditions</a>. This was done despite Libya's history of murdering dissidents abroad, and with full knowledge of Libya's brutal mistreatment of detainees.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_unrest_during_the_1990s">Political unrest during the 1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Political unrest during the 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the 1990s, Gaddafi's rule was threatened by militant <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamism</a>. In October 1993, there was an unsuccessful assassination attempt on Gaddafi by elements of the Libyan army. In response, Gaddafi used repressive measures, using his personal <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Guard_Corps" title="Revolutionary Guard Corps">Revolutionary Guard Corps</a> to crush riots and Islamist activism during the 1990s. Nevertheless, <a href="/wiki/Cyrenaica" title="Cyrenaica">Cyrenaica</a> between 1995 and 1998 was politically unstable, due to the tribal allegiances of the local troops.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="2011_civil_war_and_collapse_of_Gaddafi.27s_government">2011 civil war and collapse of Gaddafi's government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: 2011 civil war and collapse of Gaddafi's government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/2011_Libyan_civil_war" title="2011 Libyan civil war" class="mw-redirect">2011 Libyan civil war</a>, <a href="/wiki/2011_military_intervention_in_Libya" title="2011 military intervention in Libya">2011 military intervention in Libya</a>, <a href="/wiki/2011_Libyan_rebel_coastal_offensive" title="2011 Libyan rebel coastal offensive">2011 Libyan rebel coastal offensive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tripoli_(2011)" title="Battle of Tripoli (2011)">Battle of Tripoli (2011)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LibyanRepublicRelations.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/LibyanRepublicRelations.svg/220px-LibyanRepublicRelations.svg.png" width="220" height="109" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/LibyanRepublicRelations.svg/330px-LibyanRepublicRelations.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/LibyanRepublicRelations.svg/440px-LibyanRepublicRelations.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="880" data-file-height="435" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LibyanRepublicRelations.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Nations no longer recognizing (dark blue) the Great Socialist People's Libyan Arab Jamahiriya as the legitimate government of Libya. Nations that have informal relations with the National Transitional Council, but retain formal relations with the Jamahiriya, are in light blue. Nations in pink have opposed recognition of the National Transitional Council, but have not yet made a formal statement that they will not recognize it. Nations in dark red have refused to recognize the NTC, and only maintain relations with the Jamahiriya. Nations in light gray have not yet expressed a position on which government is the legitimate one.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A renewed serious threat to the Libyan Arab Jamahiriya came in February 2011, with the <a href="/wiki/2011_Libyan_Civil_War" title="2011 Libyan Civil War" class="mw-redirect">Libyan Civil War</a>. The novelist Idris Al-Mesmari was arrested hours after giving an interview with <a href="/wiki/Al_Jazeera" title="Al Jazeera">Al Jazeera</a> about the police reaction to protests in Benghazi on 15 February.</p>
<p>Inspiration for the unrest is attributed to the uprisings in <a href="/wiki/Tunisian_revolution" title="Tunisian revolution" class="mw-redirect">Tunisia</a> and <a href="/wiki/2011_Egyptian_revolution" title="2011 Egyptian revolution" class="mw-redirect">Egypt</a>, connecting it with the wider <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> On 22 February, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i> described the events as an "uprising that is trying to reclaim Libya from the world's longest-ruling <a href="/wiki/Autocracy" title="Autocracy">autocrat</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi had referred to the opposition variously as "rats", "cockroaches", and "drugged kids" and accused them of being part of <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">al-Qaeda</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the east, the <a href="/wiki/National_Transitional_Council" title="National Transitional Council">National Transitional Council</a> was established in Benghazi.</p>
<p>Gaddafi's son, <a href="/wiki/Khamis_Gaddafi" title="Khamis Gaddafi">Khamis</a>, controlled the well-armed <a href="/wiki/Khamis_Brigade" title="Khamis Brigade">Khamis Brigade</a> and alleged to possess large number of mercenaries.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Libyan_officials_who_protested_or_resigned_during_2011_protests" title="List of Libyan officials who protested or resigned during 2011 protests" class="mw-redirect">Some Libyan officials</a> had sided with the protesters and requested help from the international community to bring an end to the massacres of civilians. The government in <a href="/wiki/Tripoli" title="Tripoli">Tripoli</a> had lost control of half of Libya by the end of February,<sup id="cite_ref-aljazeeradefiant_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aljazeeradefiant-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> but as of mid-September Gaddafi remained in control of several parts of <a href="/wiki/Fezzan" title="Fezzan">Fezzan</a>. On 21 September, the forces of NTC captured Sabha, the largest city of Fezzan, reducing the control of Gaddafi to limited and isolated areas.</p>
<p>Many nations <a href="/wiki/International_reactions_to_the_2011_Libyan_civil_war" title="International reactions to the 2011 Libyan civil war" class="mw-redirect">condemned</a> Gaddafi's government over its use of force against civilians. Several other nations allied with Gaddafi called the uprising and intervention a "plot" by <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western</a> powers to loot Libya's resources.<sup id="cite_ref-Gadhafi.27s_Name_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gadhafi.27s_Name-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a> passed a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1973" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1973">resolution</a> to enforce a <a href="/wiki/No-fly_zone" title="No-fly zone">no-fly zone</a> over Libyan airspace on 17 March 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The UN resolution authorised air-strikes against Libyan ground troops and warships that appeared to threaten civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> On 19 March, the no-fly zone enforcement began, with French aircraft undertaking sorties across Libya and a naval <a href="/wiki/Blockade" title="Blockade">blockade</a> by the British <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cnn_deployment_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnn_deployment-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> Eventually, the aircraft carriers <a href="/wiki/USS_Enterprise_(CVN-65)" title="USS Enterprise (CVN-65)">USS&#160;<i>Enterprise</i></a> and <i><a href="/wiki/French_aircraft_carrier_Charles_de_Gaulle_(R91)" title="French aircraft carrier Charles de Gaulle (R91)">Charles de Gaulle</a></i> arrived off the coast and provided the enforcers with a rapid-response capability. U.S. forces named their part of the enforcement action <a href="/wiki/Operation_Odyssey_Dawn" title="Operation Odyssey Dawn">Operation Odyssey Dawn</a>, meant to "deny the Libyan regime from using force against its own people".<sup id="cite_ref-OdysseyDawn_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OdysseyDawn-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> said U.S. <a href="/wiki/Vice_Admiral" title="Vice Admiral" class="mw-redirect">Vice Admiral</a> <a href="/wiki/William_E._Gortney" title="William E. Gortney">William E. Gortney</a>. More than 110 <a href="/wiki/Tomahawk_(missile)" title="Tomahawk (missile)">"Tomahawk" cruise missiles</a> were fired in an initial assault by U.S. warships and a British submarine against Libyan air defences.<sup id="cite_ref-AlJazTanks_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AlJazTanks-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The last government holdouts in <a href="/wiki/Sirte" title="Sirte">Sirte</a> finally fell to anti-Gaddafi fighters on 20 October 2011, and, following the controversial <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Death of Muammar Gaddafi">death of Muammar Gaddafi</a>, Libya was officially declared "liberated" on 23 October 2011, ending 42 years of Gaddafi's leadership in Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Political scientist <a href="/wiki/Riadh_Sidaoui" title="Riadh Sidaoui">Riadh Sidaoui</a> suggested in October 2011 that Gaddafi "has created a great void for his exercise of power: there is no institution, no army, no electoral tradition in the country", and as a result, the period of transition would be difficult in Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Libya_under_Muammar_Gaddafi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="noprint portal tright" style="border:solid #aaa 1px;margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em">

<dl>
<dt>Institutions of governance under Gaddafi</dt>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Basic_People%27s_Congress_(country_subdivision)" title="Basic People's Congress (country subdivision)">Basic People's Congress (country subdivision)</a></li>
